===== PAGE 5 =====
Tin: XI<■ 18 I IS XOT DARK
They tell of Adam:
How frightened he must have been when, for the first time,
he saw the sun disappear, ending the light of day.
It was Adam’s first darkness.
How could he accept the night, when he had never seen a dawn?
After the splendor of the sun, how dark the darkness was for him;
how desperate the long terror of the first fall of night . . .
until Adam learned that day would come again;
could see that there is light and order in the universe.
And then Adam began to see how much light remains
in the sky at night: the stars,
and their enduring promise of the sun,
the returning star of day.
Adam learned that the night
is never wholly dark, and that no night is endless.
Even as each of us must learn it, in our own times
of trouble and of darkness.
The light is never far.
Adam’s story is our story:
Certainly, there is much that
is dark and wrong in the
world today and in ourselves:
meanness, anguish, brutality,
greed, frustration, despair.
Yet there is order and goodness
in the world and ourselves:
love, courage, sacrifice,
creativity, growth, renewal
and concern.
We have been given the
power and responsibility to
live our life as if the scales of
what is right and wrong in
the world were exactly
balanced, and our actions
change this balance.
To learn how to live this way
is not easy.
But enriched by the insights
of the past, and strengthened
by one another. . .
We are not alone.
And the light is never far.
Compliments of
LOUIS PORTNOY - WALTER PORTNOY
AND FAMILIES

Our constant gratitude, not only for
making the publication possible, but also for
the support they lend to our Youth
Activities. We urge our members to recipro¬
cate with their patronage.
• A reference and guide for the Jack¬
sonville Jewish Center membership.
• Serves over 7,000 members and ad¬
vertisers.
• All proceeds devoted to the Center’s
program.
• The only Jewish publication in the
community listing all groups.
MRS. ESTHER FISHLER
Advertising Chairman

ing us with the material used.

MESSAGE FROM OUR EXECUTIVE DIRECTOR
The President of the Jacksonville Jewish Center
has asked me to make sure that communications be¬
tween the Congregation, the Staff and the Center
Board become a priority, that the needs of the Con¬
gregation be represented and carried to the staff and
the Board members.
I am anxious to help in any way I can in the
growth and development of the Jewish Center. Please
cad me if you feel that I can be of assistance to you
and your family.
I feel certain that future additions to our congre¬
gational activities program will help meet your needs
and will go a long way in making the Center your
second home.
Sidney Cohen
JSrief Scope of ¿v4c tivities
Religious Services — Traditional prayer services are held daily, morn¬
ing and evening in the Chapel. Friday Family Services, Sabbath and
Holiday Services are observed in the Sanctuary throughout the entire
year.
Personal Religious Observances — Traditional Jewish customs are
observed in connection with Circumcision, Naming of Girls, Pidyon
Ha-ben, Bar Mitzvah, Bat Mitzvah, Confirmation, Marriages, Conver¬
sion to Judaism, Bereavement.
Education — Daily Kindergarten and Nursery with outstanding pro¬
gram of secular and religious content. Hebrew School, carefully graded
with intensive Hebrew content; Sunday School, with a curriculum in¬
cluding Jewish history and customs; Solomon Schechter Day School,
a graded secular and Hebrew School, 1st through 6th grade; with Pre-
Jr., Junior, Teen and Confirmation Congregations and Minyan Clubs.
Extension Services — Library, outstanding collection of juvenile and
adult Jewish books; Adult Education, religious and cultural Jewish
courses; Music programs of religious and cultural Jewish music; Sis¬
terhood and Men’s Clubs; Social activities provided by Mr. and Mrs.
Club, Single Adults; Family Recreation Program (Olympic Pool),
summer weekdays and Sundays for all ages at our Campsite.
Youth Activities — ATID for college age youth; United Synagogue
Youth, various age levels are served by Center Youth League, Charles
Palot Club, Pre-U.S.Y.; Scouting (Boy Scouts, Cub Scouts); Girl
Scouts; Brownies; Athletics, programs for youth and adults; Center
Day Camp & nine weeks summer program at our 36 acre Campsite on
State Road No. 13.
Family Recreation Program — Center Campsite . . . Swimming,
athletics, snack bar, available for members during the summer months.
KENNETH BOWDEN
Asst. Controller
MRS. FLORENCE ROBIN
Adm. Secretary
MRS. ESTHER FISHLER
Secretary - Year Book

===== PAGE 15 =====
The Rabbi’s Page
Dear Friends:
I feel highly privileged to be part of the re¬
newed and reinvigorated Jacksonville Jewish Cen¬
ter. Every institution has periods of stability and
periods of rapid growth. The next few years will
be, without doubt, years of rapid growth for our
congregation, and I find this to be a great chal¬
lenge for me as spiritual leader of such a growing
congregation.
The growth I refer to is not necessarily phy¬
sical growth, growth in numbers of members, (al¬
though it usually attends such growth). The
development I refer to is in spirit, in program, in
devotion, and in form. There seems to be a spirit
of renewal in the air for our synagogue center. The
excitement of all those working on our many new
programs is becoming contagious.
As I meet with our committees, as I speak to
individuals, I sense a spirit of devotion and dedi¬
cation, and a deep sense of mission. I sense a desire
to make Judaism come alive for the generation of
the 1970 as it never has before in America. I sense
an urge for a creative, vibrant, alive and dynamic
religious community.
I see this renewed vigor not only in the
growth of our Solomon Schechter Day School from
less than 100 students to almost double its former
size; not only in the rapid progress we are making
in planning for our new religious center in South
Jacksonville; not only in the growth of our staff
RABBI DOY PERETZ ELKINS
and the creativity of our programming; not only in
the youthfulness of our professional and lay lead¬
ers, but in everything that is associated with the
Jacksonville Jewish Center.
Elaine and I and our children, in the few short
months we have been here, feel that we will be
very happy in Jacksonville, working together with
all of you in helping to bring about a Jewish com¬
munity in which the ancient spirit of Judaism will
burn strongly in the hearts of all its adherents.
Faithfully,
Rabbi

===== PAGE 17 =====
Message from the President
Dear Friends:
The new decade of the seventies offers mankind
many new and exciting challenges in science, medicine,
social reform and education. Our sacred domain is that
of religion, the art of achieving the good life. Here too
we envisage new vistas to be conquered in the decade
ahead. In the words of the late Chief Rabbi of Eretz
Yisrael, Abraham Isaac Kook, “the old must be re¬
newed and the new must become sanctified.”
With our new dynamic Rabbi, Dov Peretz Elkins, our
new energetic executive director, Sidney Cohen, and
the sacred privilege and responsibility of planning and
building a new synagogue center on State Road 13, I
feel that we are on the threshold of an era unparalleled
in our Jewish community. We must all share together
the opportunity of meeting our new challenges with a
new spirit of accomplishment, of generosity, and of
dedication to the principles of our Faith.
WILBUR MARGOL, President
I know that our congregation will continue in the
year ahead to make an important contribution to Jew¬
ish life in the Jacksonville community and to the wel¬
fare of all men.
May this New Year bring you and all your loved ones good health,
contentment and peace. May mankind bring about a return to dignity for
all men and trust in God.
President
PHILIP SELBER
Chairman of the Board
On behalf of the Officers and members of the
Board of Directors and the Board of Trustees, may we
extend greetings to all of our members for a Happy
New Year, in the traditional words of
“LE SHANAH TOVAH TIKATEVU.”

===== PAGE 33 =====
The Cover of Our Year Book
STUDYING THE TORAH
WEDDING
CHANUKAH
The title of the cover picture is “KIDDUSH,”
an original color lithograph by Bertoldo Taubert of
Italy.
Bertoldo Taubert was born into an artistic family,
his father being portraitist of Victor-Emanuel, III.
In recent years, the artist has exhibited continually
in the United States at the Hammer or LeMonde
Galleries in New York, the Findley Gallery in Chi¬
cago, or the Marble Arch Gallery in Miami.
His works are warmly humanistic, and his special
additions on religious and ceremonial subjects are
in great demand.
I invite the congregation to my office at their con¬
venience, to see some of his religious pieces —-
“Studying the Torah,” “Sabbath Prayer,” “Spin¬
ning the Dreidel,” “Seder,” “Chanukah,” and “The
Wedding.”
Ben Friedman

SCHOLARSHIPS
ABRAHAM E. SELBER TRUST FUND
The Jacksonville Jewish Center formally accepted the Abraham E.
Selber Trust Fund at Confirmation Services this year. This is a $20,000
endowed scholarship to encourage students of the Center in careers such
as Rabbi, Cantor, Educator, Teacher, Youth Worker.
The Selber Trust Fund will enable the Jacksonvihe Jewish Center to
strengthen its program of assisting and encouraging high school students to
expose themselves to national programs of intensive Jewish living and learn¬
ing, such as Camp Ramah and all day religious schools.
This year students shown below received this scholarship.
Seated: Michele Ansbacher, Ruthe Lazarus and Edith Gendzier
Standing: Gary Gendzier, Craig Blattner and Philip Selber, Executor
ROBERT J. GORDON SCHOLARSHIP
Alan Gordon, son of the late Robert J. Gordon, is shown presenting a
scholarship to Jeff Cohen, this year's recipient.

CENTER CHORAL SOCIETY

no iv
The annual Music Festival was presented on Sun¬
day, February 1st at 4:00 p.m. by Cantor Abraham
Marton, the Center Choir and the Center Glee Club.
Featured soloist was Aaron Krosnick, Professor at
Jacksonville University.
The Choir presented Synagogue music by Le-
wandoski and Naumbourg as well as contemporary
composers. The Glee Club was featured in a medley
of Holiday and Israeli songs.
Throughout the year the Center Choir under the
direction of Cantor Marton provides beautiful musical
settings for Sabbath Services and for the High Holy
Days.
Soloists at the Installation Banquet: At left, Nathan Freidlin, seated at
table; Scott Saraga, Jeff Cohen, Sheila Ralph, Audrey Goldman,
Frances Lipp, Rochelle Zucker and Cantor Marton

installation
Nearly 300 persons turned out for the Installation Banquet on Monday
evening, March 30th at the Center, at which time the newly elected Board
and Officers of the Center were installed by Rabbi Dov Peretz Elkins. Mr.
Jack Price was Chairman of Arrangements and was assisted by Mrs. Jack
Price, Mrs. Hilbert Margol, in charge of decorations, Mr. and Mrs. M. D.
Evans, Mrs. Fanny Lippman and Mrs. Nathan Schneider at the door.
The U.S.Y. presented an original Cantata, the Cantor presented a
beautiful music program featuring Israeli songs and was assisted by several
children from the Glee Club.
Rabbi Elkins congratulating Wilbur Margol o nbecom-
ing President of the Jacksonville Jewish Center.
Mr. and Mrs. Nathan Freidlin shown with Mr. and
Mrs. Wilbur Margol and their daughters, Kim and
Wendy.
Mr. Philip Selber, Chairman of the Board, presenting The Auditorium on the night of the Installation
a plaque to Mr. Nathan Freidlin, outgoing President.

===== PAGE 43 =====
Ernest Goldsmith and Sheila Jacobson
appearing in “The World of Shalom Aleichem,”
sponsored by the Sisterhood.
The Jacksonville Jewish Center Sisterhood is an
affiliate of the National Women’s League, which in
turn is associated with the Jewish Theological Semi¬
nary of America.
Our program is wide in scope: We encourage adult
education through classes and study groups. Our Gift
Shop helps to introduce objects of Jewish art and
ceremony into every home. We support the building
of a secure State of Israel by means of education and
support of U.J.A. and Israel Bond drives. We try to
give guidance towards inculcating the ethics and cere¬
monies of Judaism into the home and daily life of
every Sisterhood member.
The programs at our meetings are aimed toward
teaching the beauty of our heritage. Sisterhood pro-
The cake booth at the Hanukah Fair 1969.
Program presented by the school at the Sisterhood
Hanukah Supper on December 7th.
vides funds for many vital Synagogue activities. We
finance the Sunday School teachers’ salaries, the pur¬
chase of up-to-date teaching aids, Ramah scholarships,
youth programs such as U.S.Y. and ATID, as well as
the Scout troops. Whenever the Center needs new
furnishings or kitchen equipment, the Sisterhood is
called upon. Equally important, Sisterhood provides
woman power to support many of these valuable pro¬
grams.
Sisterhood is dedicated to the perpetuation of a
modern Judaism rooted in tradition, and the trans¬
lation of its high ideals into practice through a living
Judaism in the home, the Synagogue, and the com¬
munity.
Flag raising by the Brownie Troop
at the Hanukah Supper.

This year we will completely re-organize the Men’s Club and hope to
have a full and successful program.
Both the Rabbi and the Executive Director will play an important part
in this vital organization. We are looking forward to many programs featur¬
ing outstanding speakers and panel discussions.
As always, the major concern of the Men’s Culb has been the youth of
the Center. We hope to sponsor events to improve our facilities, promote
Boy Scouts and other youth organizations, and provide a social outlet for
our members of the synagogue.

aintenance
JiitcL
en
Annie Mae Fort Tim Riley
Our maintenance staff shoulders one of the heaviest burdens in our
Center program, that of maintaining our facilities so that they are always
in a presentable state for our use and for the various activities at the Center.
Pictured are Tim Riley and Annie Mae Fort, both of whom have been
loyal and devoted members of our maintenance crew for many years. Annie
Mae Fort also maintains the Kitchen and prepares many mea^s.
The local caterers, Mr. and Mrs. Meyerhoff and Mrs. Mary Safer pre¬
pare many Simchas for our members and their families.
Our appreciation to this loyal and devoted staff who have given many
years of unselfish service to the Jewish Center.
Shown above is the newest addition to our fleet of buses. We have
three large vehicles like the one above and one small bus.
Our staff of drivers is headed by Morris Montford, a twenty year plus
veteran, who has an enviable record. He teams with the other two drivers
to transport our students from their homes and schools to classes at the
Center.
Herbert Lewitt is Chairman of the Transportation Committee which
supervises the system of routes, under the direction of Kenneth Bowden.

MRS. NATHAN SANDLER,
Principal of Foundation School
TED GROSS,
Principal of
Religious School
There is an old expression in Jewish life, that the synagogue —
Bet Knesseth — is to be used for three functions: as a House of
Prayer, of Sociability, and of study. Without a strong link within these
three functions, the total structure becomes weak. The School De¬
partment felt in respect to study and prayer there has been a tendency
to relegate these activities to the young, mainly. To cope with this
problem the school has striven to reach as many individual students
as possible. Where these contacts were made, the results were excellent.
The most gratifying of all is the fact that our standards have risen
in respect to Bar Mitzvah qualifications, and have opened the way to
the biggest expansion our school has seen in any one year for the Solo¬
mon Schechter School. With five grades for the coming year, and a
definite commitment for a sixth grade next year, we can look forward
to a greater achievement in both areas mentioned above. With the new
rabbinical leadership we will again be in a position to cement relaton-
ships in all areas.
Loyalty is difficult to measure and cannot be repaid. It is with
deep gratitude that we thank the past Chairman of the Education
Committee, Mr. Jack Shorstein, and all the people who served with
him, and welcome Mr. Sheldon Gendzier as the new Chairman, who
has already affected much of our planning in the expanding program.
Thanks also to all the members of the staff in the various departments,
and in the office.
MRS. ALFRED ROSENZWEIG,
School Secretary
SHELDON GENDZIER,
Education Chairman

YOUTH ACTIVITIES
MRS. LOUIS SLOTT
Youth Activities Chairman
CENTER YOUTH ORGANIZATIONS
Center Youth League (C.Y.L.) — A coed United
Synagogue Youth Chapter for students in the
10th, 11th and 12th grades. Meetings are held
on the second and fourth Sunday of each month.
Charles Palot Club (C.P.C.) — U.S.Y. Chapter for
8th and 9th grade boys and girls. Meetings on
the second and fourth Sunday of each month.
Kadimah U.S.Y. — A coed club for 7th grade stu¬
dents with varied social, religious and athletic
functions held monthly.
Boy Scouts — Troop 14 — Meetings on Tuesday
nights at 8:00 at the Center. Patrol meetings
are held weekly in neighborhoods.
Cub Scouts — Pack 14 —
Den Meetings held weekly in neighborhoods.
Pack Meetings — last Thursday of each month
at 7:30 P.M. at the Center.
Brownies — Troop 147 — Meetings every Friday.
Junior Girl Scouts — Troop 162 — Meetings every
Friday.
Cadet Girl Scouts — Troop — Meetings every
Monday, 7:00.
JERRY KREMER
Chairman of Youth Commission

The Center Youth League, high school group of
United Synagogue of America, brings together Jewish
youth who really care about Judaism and other people
in the world. We learn more about ourselves, enabling
us to overcome problems which we encounter. Most
of all, U.S.Y. builds trust and faith; a trust in Judaism
and faith in our fellowman.
BRUCE HOROVITZ
U.S.Y. cannot make a devoted Jew. Only the in¬
dividual can do that. U.S.Y. can only strengthen the
bonds between individuals and Judaism. That bond
can only be as strong as desired. Socially U.S.Y. can
help greatly. It gathers Jewish youth to join in con¬
ventions, dances and parties. This past year we trav¬
eled to Miami for Regional Convention. Jacksonville
U.S.Y. also went to St. Petersburg for another con¬
vention. In sports action, U.S.Y. trounced A.Z.A. in a
basketball game, 37-36. The game was played in the
Center Gym and was thoroughly enjoyable and excit¬
ing.
This past year we had many interesting discus¬
sions. Some examples: “Interdating,” “The Draft,”
“Our Laws - Do They Make Sense?”, “Soviet Jewry”
and “Is Chanukah Becoming the Jewish Christmas?”
I would like to thank Ricki Gross, Bruce Horo¬
vitz, Larry Browdy, Shirley Berman, Elaine Goldstein,
Mark Gendzier and Mark Kessler for their help and
leadership. I would also like to thank Mrs. Sheldon
Gendzier for her help and understanding.
Finally, I would like to thank everyone who was a
member in U.S.Y. I appreciate your choosing me as
President to lead you this past year, and I really hope
that we all received a greater knowledge and under¬
standing in anything we undertake.
Shalom,
Sammy Slom, Past President
Mark Gendzier and Shirley Berman,
Outstanding Boy and Girl
Standing — Rubin Saraga, Bruce Horovitz, Mark
Gendzier and Shelly Werner, Advisor.
Seated — Mark Shorstein, Scott Zimmerman and
Janice Mizrahi.

EDITH GENDZIER
CHARLES PALOT CLUB
To Jewish youth, United Synagogue Youth
should be an unforgettable experience, and it is the
job of the officers to successfully mix social and study
so that it is interesting and enjoyable to the U.S.Y.
members.
The programs for the past year have been both
informative and entertaining. We started our pro¬
gramming for the year with a movie from the Bell Sys¬
tem about the FUTURE. After the movie we were
served refreshments in a room decorated with tinfoil
and lights to give us a futuristic effect.
An excellent program was presented on the drug
problem when the speaker, Mr. Jabo Cox, an ex-drug
addict relayed his experiences.
Some of the other programs this year were square
dancing, Soviet Jewry, an Israeli Festival, and a trip
to the Zoo.
In April, our Club sponsored a Rally in Hemming
Park to protest the oppression of the Soviet Jews. We
Karen Gartner presenting
outstanding trophy to
Mark Gendzier
had several speakers including Mr. Jack Becker, Mr.
Jack Coleman, Mr. Ted Gross and Mr. Ben Fried¬
man. The event was covered on Channel 4 and an
article placed in the Jacksonville Journal.
Study groups also play a very important part in
U.S.Y. activities. To highlight our study group of the
year, Mr. Joel Goldman discussed with us the topic:
“Is Man Basically Evil?” The session was held after
Shabbat Services and dinner. We discussed criminal
instincts and whether they were inborn or acquired.
We finished with no definite conclusion, but we did
gain some insight into the subject.
I would like to thank Jeri Essock, all my officers,
and the members for making this a successful year in
C.P.C. I especially want to thank Mrs. Slott for her
encouragement and guidance throughout the year.
On behalf of the officers and members of the
Charles Palot Club I would like to extend wishes for
a Happy New Year in a world of Peace!
Mark Shorstein, President
Standing — Craig Blattner, Geri Essock, Advisor;
Mrs. Blanche Slott, Advisor and Mitchell Kowitz.
Seated — Edith Gendzier, Rochelle Zucker, Ruthe
Lazarus, Michele Ansbacher and Deena Safer.

“On my honor I will do my best to do my duty to
God and my country and to obey the Scout Law; to
help other people at all times; to keep myself physically
strong, mentally awake, and morally straight.”
With these words, Boy Scouts of Troop 14 dedi¬
cate themselves to the principles of the Boy Scouts of
America, an organization which has continued to grow
since its birth in 1909 to over 33 million boys today.
Troop 14, the third troop to be organized in Jack¬
sonville, celebrated its Fiftieth Anniversary on Febru¬
ary 22, 1970. At this time honors were given to all
Scoutmasters, Charter members and Eagle Scouts.
1969-70 was a very busy year for Troop 14. Led
by Scoutmaster Stephen Hackel, members of the
Troop participated in camping trips to Anastasia State
Park and the Center Southside Site where they ac¬
quired the knowledge necessary for advancements.
Scouting is available for all boys between the ages
of 11 through high school. Weekly meetings were held
at the Center and patrols met with their leaders in
various neighborhoods..

We are proud of our Cub Pack 14 for the out¬
standing role it plays in helping young boys eight and
nine years of age to become good citizens to become
physically fit and morally strong in this fast moving
age when they are exposed to many temptations.
Pack 14 has had continuous charter renewal for
the past 31 years and during the year shared in many
new and different experiences each month, including
a Carnival, Folklore Pinewood Derby, Circus, Nature,
participation in Scout Sabbath Services, Arts and
Crafts and the city-wide Scout-a-rama. All boys aided
in the Goodwill Drive by distributing bags and picking
up articles for the needy.
Always an important part of the Cub Scout year
is the annual Blue and Gold Banquet at which time
awards and acknowledgments are given. This coming
year will initiate the presentation of the Larry Hacker
award to the outstanding Cub.
Many dedicated people deserve our thanks for
their outstanding service: Sidney Engelhardt, Cub-
master, Mrs. Fred Kupperman, Den Mother Coach and
the Den Mothers, Mrs. Bennie Margol, Mrs. Julian
Moss, Mrs. Lester Greenfield, Mrs. Harry Schemer,
and Mrs. Fred Kupperman; Institutional Representa¬
tive Henry Lasris and members of the Troop Com¬
mittee; Charles Mizrahi, Chairman, Ted Freedman and
Stanley Ruby.

The events of the year were as follows: Regular
meeting in September; Dedication in October, Vet¬
erans’ Day Parade in November, Hanukah Party
in December, Camping one weekend in January,
Daughter-Father Banquet in February, Girl Scout
Sabbath in March, Camping one weekend in April
and Silver Springs Trip in May.
The Troop did service projects for Sisterhood,
the Jewish Family Services and the River Garden
Home, and planted trees at th Campsite. The
Troop also sold 2781 boxes of cookies in the yearly
sale, placing second in the Gateway Council, and
enabling the girls to pay for the Silver Springs
Trip. Troop 162 also received the Pier Award.

(Center
MRS. BLANCHE SLOTT
The Center Day Camp located on the Center's
Southside Site at 10149 State Road No. 13 has been
in continuous operation since 1946.
During nine weeks of the summer, children of the
Center membership and of the entire Jewish commu¬
nity have available to them the finest facilities for fun
and growth in this area.
The varied program of activities include swimming
in the Olympic pool, skiing, all active sports, arts and
crafts, singing and dancing, horseback riding, field
trips to places of interest, ice skating and roller skat¬
ing.
During the past summer names of the groups were
changed to represent the Tribes of Israel and most at¬
tractive identification, were placed in the area of each
group.
Jewish values are an integral part of the Camp pro¬
gram and campers participate in the blessings before
and after meals, enjoy a Shabbat program, complete
with Kiddush, challah, Israeli singing and dancing.
Tisha B’AB was observed during the morning with a
presentation by Rabbi Elkins which made all aware of
the importance of remembering this tragic day in the
history of the Jewish people.
We look forward to many new concepts for the
Day Camp in 1971 and hope that you will include our
Camp in the summer plans for your children.

ctics
EDWARD WITTEN
Ch. Athletics Comm.
The athletic program of the Center this past year
was one of fun and enjoyment. The men’s basketball
team competed in the Southside Church League and
placed second in the league with an overall record of
13 victories and 7 defeats.
The men’s softball team, a new addition to last
year’s activities proved to be quite a successful unit.
The team participated in the Southeast Church league
placing second overall and compiling a record of 26
victories and 6 defeats. The team was invited to the
Metro tournament but because of the shortness of time
as to when the team was to play the team was unable
to participate.
The future for the athletic program is unlimited
but unfortunately it will only be as good as you the
members make it. We want everyone to keep in mind
the availability to all members the facilities of the
Center and the Campsite. Keep fit!!! Join in the ac¬
tive participation of the Center teams.

^AVS'Jb
ATID is the College Age Organization of Conser¬
vative Judaism sponsored by the United Synagogue
of America. It was formed and is led by college stu¬
dents and college-agers who want to live and learn
Judaism and continue their identification with the
Conservative Movement.
ATID invites those who are between 18 and 24 who
wish to involve themselves in Jewish life and thought.
ATID Chapters are formed on campuses or within
Conservative Congregations.
Local ATIDniks had a most successful summer
with get-togethers each Wednesday at different homes
with varied and informative topics discussed as: Black
Heritage, Archeology in Israel, Jewish Defense League,
Suicide Prevention, Soviet Anti-Semitism and the
Arab Refugee Problem.
Members also participated in the Shabbat Services
at the Center Southside Site on August 8th and ended
the season with a well attended social affair.
KAREN GARTNER,
Chairman
This outstanding summer would not have been
possible without the guidance of Rabbi Elkins and the
excellent work done by the Chairman, Karen Gartner,
and the Secretary, Susan Gendzier.
Mike Backer, Judy Uditsky, Karen Gartner,
Susan Gendzier and Barry Setzer

The Mr. and Mrs. Club in its eighteenth success¬
ful year promotes friendship among married couples.
Each month exciting and novel affairs are held with
committees formed to arrange each event. The themes
vary from month to month, but include an extravagant
New Year’s Eve party, mid-year Installation Banquet
and a picnic or outing during the summer.
All Couples between thirty-five and fifty years
of age interested in sharing good and friendly compan¬
ionship are urged to contact any club officer or board
member.

The N.E.T.W.O.R.K. is an organization providing
both cultural and social activities for the single Jewish
community within the age group of 18-35.
Membership consists of a yearly fee plus a nominal
charge for each social affair. Projected plans call for
one party each month, complete with entertainment,
a weekend at Daytona Beach, including young adults
from other cities and periodic mailings to keep mem¬
bers informed of all activities.
All young adults are welcome to join, meet new
people and renew old friendships. For further infor¬
mation, please contact any of the above members of
the Board of Directors.

The Tofield Memorial Library is a depository of
books, information and materials of Jewish interest
for knowledge, recreation and leisure, and appreciation
of the arts. To insure that all holdings are easily ac¬
cessible and available to every member of the congre¬
gation, the library has been kept open every day of ehe
week, including Friday evening and Saturday after¬
noon after services.
The Sisterhood - Library Oneg Shabbat occurred
during Jewish Book Month. It was an enjoyable pro¬
gram. Appropriate book lists were distributed and at¬
tractive book displays were made to encourage con¬
gregation interest and participation.
In cooperation with the Youth Activities Program
of the Center, a “Story Hour” was held in the library
on Saturday afternoons. This was well received.

THE RECREATION GAZEBO
The Center Southside site is open for family recreation throughout the
summer months. For a nominal charge, members of the community may use
all facilities of the site which include an Olympic swimming pool, complete
with dressing rooms and sun bathing areas, a well equipped children’s play¬
ground, tennis, basketball and handball courts and shaded picnic areas. Also
available are two ball fields, modern snack bar and recreational pavillion.
Many families took advantage of this wonderful program of family re¬
laxation by swimming, playing and picnicking together. The highlight of the
summer was a men’s handball tournament which offered much fun and ex¬
citement to the many participants and spectators. First place trophy win¬
ners were Stanley Kantor and Dr. Ronald Levin. Runners-up were Bernard
Brust and Warren Sefton.
The 1971 season will offer many new and interesting family activities.
Plan now to participate!

I am looking forward to meeting all of our youth
groups and plan with them our athletic programs.
Participation in athletics and teaching have been
great sources of joy to me. I know there is great in¬
terest in revitalizing our athletic program at the Jack¬
sonville Jewish Center. With the help of the youth
leaders and parents, it will be done.
On Sunday afternoons there will be a basketball
clinic from the Fifth grade through the Tenth grade.
From the clinic I will form teams and a league to play
in. We hope we will have a representative team of 11th
and 12th graders to play other organizations.
If we have enough girls participating in volleyball
and tennis, we will also have teams and a league. In
the spring we will start our softball teams.
Let’s look forward to an exciting year in athletics
at the Center and remember its importance.
Sidney Cohen,
Executive Director
our

assistants, Herbert Gordon and Jack Schemer.

SCENE AT CENTER CEMETERY
Selection of Memorial Property
Family ownership of memorial property simply makes good sense. The Center has plenty of family lots available and it
is not likely that the cost will decrease. As a matter of fact, with the increasing costs of maintenance, prices will likely increase.
Those of us who have seen a cemetery lot selected at a time of immediate need, perhaps by the widow, or by a son, daugh¬
ter, know that this is the worst possible time. There are too many other items demanding attention and it is difficult to give
the matter proper consideration. A cemetery lot should not be selected hastily. Since fate makes no appointments, the best time
to purchase family memorial space is when you DON’T need it.
Visit our Memorial Park on 40th Street east of Main Street. Look around. We take pride in the fact that the property is
well tended. Then, at your convenience, call the Center and arrangements can be made to meet and proceed with the space se¬
lection under ideal conditions.

Serving the entire Jewish community of Jackson¬
ville, the Jewish Community Council is the central
Welfare Fund and Community Relations body of the
Jewish community. An association of individuals,
synagogues, organizations and social service agencies,
the program encompasses key activities which give
unity, cohesion and coordination to local Jewish com¬
munal life.
A community-wide annual Welfare Fund Cam¬
paign provides support to significant local, national
and overseas organizations. The National United Jew¬
ish Appeal has expressed American Jewry’s respon¬
sibility for the welfare and rehabilitation needs of
Jewish communities the world over for over 200 years.
River Garden Hebrew Home for the Aged and the
Jewish Family and Children’s Services are the local
beneficiaries.
The Council publishes a monthly newspaper known
as the Commentator. It is the official organ of the
Council and its member organizations.
The Council conducts a leisure time activities
program which includes Youth Council programs such
as college preparatory workshops, a youth news page
in the Commentator, and a youth division in the an¬
nual Jewish Welfare Fund Campaign.

In its 53rd year, at the beginning of a new de¬
cade, the Jewish Family & Children’s Services, Inc.
strives to put into practice the deeply ingrained con¬
cern for one’s fellow man that led to its founding, and
to use the current wisdom to overcome the perennial
problems that face us in old and new forms. With a
continuing inflation, a tighter labor market and a
slowing down of business, the limited financial pro¬
gram offered markedly shot up in the first half of the
year.
The search, by young people for new ways, their
emphasis on Telling it as it is’, and confrontation ex¬
posed communication gaps between the generations
and in family therapy, where all the members of a
household sit down to work through the conflicting
values, interests and means of expression, the sessions
often were more explosive but also deeper.
While still waiting for the effects of the liberaliza¬
tion of abortion laws in the various states and the
major revisions of the old welfare system, the minor
changes involving residency and Medicaid, so far, have
had little effect on the basic program of the agency
but a careful eye is being kept to adjust the program
to meet the needs of the Jewish community on the
highest level.
To meet the needs the agency is currently offer¬
ing:
1. Family & Individual Counseling.
2. Vocational and Employment Counseling.
3. Service to Senior Citizens in their own
homes.
4. River Garden admissions.
5. Adoption Program.
6. Foster Home Care.
7. Short term financial assistance.
8. Transient Service.
9. Services to Prisoners in Raiford.
10. Services to Patients at Macclenny.
11. Day Camp and College loan program.
12. Friendly Visiting Program.
If a personal crisis should confront you or if you
would like to help someone with problems, you will be
assured of a warm, confidential, and professional re¬
sponse.

March 22, 1970 marked the date for the dedica¬
tion of the Eugenia Coleman addition to the Home.
The decor, furnishings and layout creates a cli¬
mate of quiet comfort for the more than 80 residents
now living there.
Donald Cohn Vice President
Co-Chairman Admissions Board

Co-Chairman Admissions Board

The overall capacity of the Home is 185 beds,
divided into 6 nursing units averaging 30 residents per
unit. The accent is on the wellness of the person rather
than his illness.
The Home is accredited by the Joint Commission
of Hospital and Related Health Institutions and ap¬
proved for Medicare and Medicaid patients by the
Division of Health and Rehabilitation of the State of
Florida.
The Home’s emphasis is on a total Jewish way
of life to substitute for the family who is no longer
able to care for their aged. The Synagogue, the Holi¬
day observations, and the Sabbath observation creates
this climate of Jewishness.
The other components of human needs are the
social and personal relationships which develop from
sharing with another human being.
A broad range of programs, arts and crafts and
even drama, produces this sense of sharing and belong¬
ing. This year’s production of Carmen Shapiro by the
River Garden Players was as well received as last
year’s Fiddler on the Roof.

This is the beginning of the 25th year of our serv¬
ices to the aging of Florida. There has been a rather
significant growth of the Home since March, 1946
when it first opened its doors.
With the opening of the Coleman addition, River
Garden can anticipate even greater achievement in
this our Silver Anniversary year.

River Garden Auxiliary was founded 47 years
ago, and today stands as the largest Jewish women’s
organization in Jacksonville, with over 800 members.
A group of dedicated women dreamed of having a
home for the Jewish aged, and, after many years of
devoted service, their dream became a reality in 1946
when the doors of River Garden Hebrew Home for
the Aged was officially opened.
Last fall the Eugenia Coleman Memorial Build¬
ing opened its doors to accomodate 90 additional resi¬
dents. This magnificent building, with its added space,
will allow River Garden a greater degree of versatility
in the type of care given its residents.
The women of the auxiliary still provide service
with a heart through our Red Cross volunteers, Golden
Sunshine Club drivers, who provide door to door trans¬
portation for non residents so they can attend the
weekly meetings held at River Garden. We also bring
entertainment and refreshments to the residents on
special holidays, and participate in such projects as
ceramic and art classes, hobby shop and residents’
magazine.
Mrs. Myron Rosenberg, Mrs. H. C. Sims,
Mrs. Gerald Glickstein and Mrs. Sol Schauer
In the new building we are engaged in raising
money for decorating and furnishing, through our
Tombola, Donor and Membership drives. Further
funds are raised through our Social Printing Projects.
The annual Linen Shower helps replenish linens at the
Home.
With the expansion program reaching its fulfill¬
ment, the responsibilities of River Garden Auxiliary
have reached a new magnitude. We pledge to continue
our efforts and cooperation in bringing not only ma¬
terial, but spiritual values as well, into the lives of our
elder citizens.

This is the year of opportunity and challenge for
the Z.O.A. The American President has confirmed that
the continued existence of a strong Israel is of vital
interest to the United States. However, there are
strong pressure groups in this country (including the
oil interests and local and foreign Arabs) who seek to
discredit Israel and deprive her of American support.
It is the responsibility of the Z.O.A. to oppose the
untruth propaganda of these pressure groups and to
educate the American population, both Jewish and
Gentile, to the fact that America needs a strong Israel
almost as much as Israel needs a strong America. This
can be accomplished through a Speakers Bureau and
through Committees we have organized, together with
Hadassah, to answer the lying letters written by local
Arabs which regularly appear in the Times-Union. We
are also planning other action on this subject.
DAVID GOODMAN,
President
The Z.O.A. this year wi]l seek to educate at least
the Jewish community in regard to these companies
which submit to the Arab boycott against Israel.
The Z.O.A. will interest itself in promoting the
sale of Israeli goods and services. For example, if
American Jews would use El A1 more in their travels
to Europe, this would be of great benefit to Israel.
There are many Israeli products for sale at the Cen¬
ter, the Temple and Etz Chaim which should be
brought to the attention of our Jewish population.
Also, there are fine Israeli made clothes to be pur¬
chased in local stores. Israeli trade must be greatly
increased.
Z.O.A. supports magnificent camps in Israel which
American children would greatly enjoy. All American
Jews benefit from the work of the Z.O.A.

Unique for its achievement, dynamism and for its
service in Israel and the U.S., Hadassah, the Women's
Zionist Organization of America, continues to make
records in all fields in which it is engaged. Founded
in 1912 by Miss Henrietta Szold, Hadassah has grown
to the largest women's organization in the world.
The Hadassah program began as a modest at¬
tempt to raise the health standards of what was, at
that time, Palestine. It now conducts an internation¬
ally renowned program of healing, teaching and med¬
ical research, child rescue work, vocational education,
social welfare and land redemption in Israel.
In the United States, Hadassah seeks to educate
its members to become better informed American cit¬
izens, to live more creatively as part of the American
Jewish community and to work actively for world
peace.
New and exciting on Hadassah's horizon is their
Mrs. Lewis Meisel, President;
Mrs. Raymond Roberts and Mrs. Ronald Levin
community American Affairs program. Realizing the
profound need at this time for social action, the pro¬
gram seeks to alleviate hunger within the community
and work with learning problems within the schools.
Hadassah meets the second Tuesday of each
month from September through May. The Hadassah
Bazaar which is staffed by volunteer workers is lo¬
cated on 411 Main Street.

This evening group of Hadassah is a vital part of the Jacksonville1 Chapter.
It provides opportunity for active participation in Hadassah work to those
women who are unable to attend day-time meetings. The programs for each
meeting are carefully planned to include educational and fund raising
themes. They feature interesting speakers, movies, and book reviews. A so¬
cial hour follows each meeting and newcomers and guests are always
welcome.

LEONARD COHN
The year 1969 has been a successful one for A. B.
Weil Lodge. During the year, a further study of Hillel
on the J. U. campus was made, and as a result we
hope to offer substantially greater assistance to this
group in the coming college year.
Our local A. B. Weil scholarship fund was again
instrumental in helping to provide a college education
to some of our local youngsters, who might otherwise
not have been able to continue with their education;
and we hope to expand our activities in this field in
the coming year and thereby offer assistance to a
greater number of prospective college students.
The A.D.L. committee has substantiaUy increased
its efforts due to the rising tensions and pressures at
home and abroad. This being an election year, the
problems are readily evident.
There has been a renewed interest in the Lodge
by many of our more reliable stalwarts, ably assisted
by some of our more active newer workers; and as a
result, it appears that the new slate of officers will be
able to show a very substantial increase in member¬
ship, along with increased activity and accomplishment
in the regular endeavors of the A. B. Weil Lodge.

Lodge of B’nai B’rith for inclusion in your Year Book

B’nai B’rith Women are dedicated to serving you, your
family, your country and humanity. This is carried through
by supporting different national B’nai B’rith philanthropies
such as Bellefaire Children’s Center; Leo N. Levi Memorial
Hospital; National Jewish Hospital and B’nai B’rith Center
for Jewish patients at Mayo Clinic. B’nai B’rith Women also
sponsors the Children’s Home in Israel and other Israeli pro¬
jects. Locally, our activities include: The Anti-Defamation
League (“Dolls for Democracy” program, distribution of memo
calendar books to the public schools and keeping members
aware of the problems in the Jewish Community); helping at
River Garden Home; sponsoring the B.B. Girls; Hillel at U.
of Fla.; and helping in all community drives. In the coming
year we hope to make the nam B’NAI B’RITH synonymous
with our motto: Service, Fidelity and Devotion.
“Dolls for Democracy” project of B’nai B’rith Women
will bring historical figures into local schools. Here Mrs.
Gus Goldstein, President, shows part of collection to Mrs.
Murray Starr, left, and Mrs. Harry Glenn, right. Dolls shown
represent Chaim Weizmann, St. Francis, Sister Kenny, George
Washington Carver and Eleanor Roosevelt.

Shalom,
Another year for Gators BBG passes. As we look
back on this year we would like to tell people of our
discoveries.
This has been a rough year for all and especially
our Past President. We had a Spaghetti Supper to
raise money for our yearly Muscular Dystrophy Drive.
At this supper we raised close to $200.00. Also at the
end of the year we received bad news that one of our
advisors were leaving us as she was moving to Miami.
Mrs. Hoffman, we would like to thank you for every¬
thing you did for us and lots of luck with your new
member of the family. With this we would like to wel¬
come our new advisor, Mrs. Louise Axelberg, a past
member of BBG.
Later on in the year we went to conventions for
which we received an award and also a President of
Council.
There is a saying in BBG.
IDENTITY, IDENTITY
WHO AM I?
I’M A BBG
I’M THE ONE
THE ONLY ONE
THERE IS SO MUCH I CAN DO.
So come join us and our brother chapter and make
this year the best of all and see what you can do.

The National Council of Jewish Women is an organiza¬
tion which, in the spirit of Judaism is dedicated to further¬
ing human welfare in the Jewish and general communities,
locally, nationally, and internationally. Through an integrated
program of education, service and social action, it provides
essential services and stimulates and educates the individual
and the community toward their responsibility in advancing
human welfare and the democratic way of life.
It is in the spirit of Faith and Humanity that the Jackson¬
ville Section, Inc., carries out the following projects in our
community.
1. Mental Health Forum — Our annual program and sem¬
inar, jointly sponsored with the Mental Health Associa¬
tion of Duval County, increases public understanding in
this field.
2. Medical Loan Shelf — We supply hospital and sick room
equipment to the indigent of our community at no cost
to the individual. We are the only source of this equipment
in the community at the present time.
3. Pre~School Vision Screening — Our trained volunteers
test the eyes of Pre-School Children in Duval County for
Amblyopia (lazy-eye blindness). Private kindergartens,
Federal and GJEO school are included. We were the first of
several participating organizations to conduct this service.
4. Teacher s Aid — Our trained members aid classroom teach¬
ers with academic instructions of children with speech and
hearing handicaps.
Mrs. Joseph Mason
5. Senior Citizens Program — Under the direction and sup¬
ervision of Council volunteers, residents of the Garden
Home make hand-sewn layettes, which are distributed
through local welfare agencies for needy families in our
community.
7. Overseas Program — Through the Ship-a-Box Committee,
we send work and play materials to the kindergartens in
Israel. This is done by contributions of toys from our mem¬
bers and the children of the Sunday Schools of our Jewish
Community.
6. V.I.P S. — Volunteers in Public Schools.
8. Project ‘'PACT” — (Portable Articles for Children and
Teachers) Working in cooperation with the Children’s
Museum, Council volunteers will assembly portable dis¬
plays to be presented in the classrooms of the Duval Coun¬
ty schools, to augment their Social Studies program. It is
for the project, that the Jacksonville Section of National
Council of Jewish Women was given the 1970 “EVE”
Award, in the field of Education, by the Florida Publish¬
ing Company.

(Organization for Rehabilitation through Training)
OFFICERS 1970-71

The President and Vice-Presidents of O.R.T.
Women’s American ORT, while only 5 years old
in Jacksonville, has been in the United States since
1924, and has been a national organization for 90
years.
O.R.T. is a program for teaching people, young
and old, vocational skills so they can make their own
way in life. It is the CHARITY TO END ALL CHAR¬
ITY.
Briefly, the O.R.T. Program consists of:
• 703 Trade & Technical Training Centers.
• Located in 23 countries, including North Amer¬
ica, South America, Iran, India, France, Italy,
and Israel.
• Approximately 50,000 persons are helped an¬
nually.
• Teaching staff, mostly O.R.T. graduates, num¬
bers about 1,700.
• O.R.T. schools teach 72 different job skills.
• Annual cost of this program is in excess of
$15,000,000.
• Largest program is in Israel, with over 35,000
trainees in 72 schools.
One of the most exciting projects that O.R.T. is
entering into is the O.R.T. School of Engineering to
be built on the campus of the Hebrew University. Con¬
struction is about to begin, and students will be able
to attend in September of 1971. With Israel’s need
for technician and engineers becoming so much greater
as each day passes, this school, as are all of our
schools, will be a tremendous asset to the economy and
well-being of the state of Israel.
To help meet our financial assignments locally,
we conduct fund-raising projects throughout the year,
the largest of these being our Chinese Auction which
is held annually in the Wolf son High Cafeteria. Also
on the agenda is an Art Auction to be held in Septem¬
ber. Of every Dollar raised through these affairs, 90%
goes to the O.R.T. Program.
For every two people joining O. R. T., another
child can be accepted in an O.R.T School somewhere.
Our Open meetings are usually the last Monday of
each month, September through May. Won’t you JOIN
us?

The Progressive Credit Union sponsored by the
Jewish Progressive Society is organized to encourage
thrift and provide a safe, convenient medium where¬
by members can invest and accumulate savings. The
Credit Union carries life savings insurance of $2,000
and loan protection insurance without any direct cost
to the members. Last year the Board declared a 3%
dividend on savings and 10% rebate of interest to
borrowing members.
Ten Commandments of our Credit Union: The
Member is the most important person in our Credit
Union. The Member is not an outsider, he is an owner
of our Credit Union. The Member is not an interrup¬
tion of our work, he is the purpose of it. The Member
does us a favor when he calls — We are not doing
him a favor when we serve him. The Member is not
a cold statistic — He is a human being with feelings
and emotions like our own. The Member is not some¬
one to argue or match wits with. The Member is a
person who brings us his wants — it is our job to
fill these wants. The Member is deserving of the most
courteous and attentive treatment we can give him.
The Member is not dependent o nus, we are dependent
on him. The Member is the life blood of this Credit
Union.
The year’s activities climax with the annual dinner
meeting at the Center. Anyone who wishes to join is
extended a cordial invitation.
OUR WORSHIP IN
A child who is born a Jew
Is as sweet as a honey-dew.
From the cradle to the nursing
He is loved always and no cursing.
A toy with which to play
This is his joy in his way.
Who knows his future
Is a secret hidden in nature.
He runs around the Earth
To acquire only what he deserves.
He follows the studying of Torah
To enlighten his heart, as bright as the Menorah.
He is great in business and longs
To reach the heights as King Solomon.
THE HOLY TEMPLE
He tries to be a Rabbi
To be saved in the Hochanah-Rabbi.
We all come to worship in the Temple
With bowing head and heart atremble.
O Lord, hear our prayer
Renew our heart with a new layer.
Your Holy Commandments rule us
As an obedient child that loves us.
In closing our year in Yom Kippur
A Holy Spirit covers us to make us pure.
We pray for our ancestors that are living in Soul
As all of us and the child to reach his goal.
Todah—Rabbi
To all our Rabbis.
By Isaac M. Kabbani

(Passover), the Feast
2 IYAR
of Unleavened Bread
3 IYAR
— an eight-day
4 IYAR
celebration of the
L. C. 6:46 p.m.
5 IYAR
deliverance of the
Jews from
h
Egyptian slavery. During the traditional Seder meal, on the iirst two nights of Passover, Jewish families read the Hag-
gadah (a ritual recitation of the story of the Exodus). Throughout the Passover periods, foods are selected according
to special dietary laws. Instead of bread, Jews eat matzos (unleavened bread) to recall the hasty flight from Egypt,
when there was not time to allow the bread to rise.
